		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 82px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 80px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Keep_your_rosaries_graffiti.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Feminist graffiti in A Coruña, Spain, that reads Enough with rosaries in our ovaries"><img alt="Feminist graffiti in A Coruña, Spain, that reads Enough with rosaries in our ovaries" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Keep_your_rosaries_graffiti.jpg/120px-Keep_your_rosaries_graffiti.jpg" decoding="async" width="80" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Keep_your_rosaries_graffiti.jpg/179px-Keep_your_rosaries_graffiti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Keep_your_rosaries_graffiti.jpg/239px-Keep_your_rosaries_graffiti.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="3008" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext"><a href="/wiki/Female_graffiti_artists" title="Female graffiti artists">Feminist graffiti</a> in <a href="/wiki/A_Coru%C3%B1a" title="A Coruña">A Coruña</a>, Spain, that reads <i>Enough with rosaries in our ovaries</i></div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 164.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 162.66666666667px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Berliner_Mauer.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Berlin Wall: &quot;Anyone who wants to keep the world as it is, does not want it to remain&quot;"><img alt="Berlin Wall: &quot;Anyone who wants to keep the world as it is, does not want it to remain&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Berliner_Mauer.jpg/250px-Berliner_Mauer.jpg" decoding="async" width="163" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Berliner_Mauer.jpg/500px-Berliner_Mauer.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="680" data-file-height="502" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext"><a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wall" title="Berlin Wall">Berlin Wall</a>: "Anyone who wants to keep the world as it is, does not want it to remain"</div>
		</li>
</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Genocide_denial">Genocide denial</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1251242444" /><table class="box-Undue_weight plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-undue-weight" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span class="skin-invert-image" typeof="mw:File"><span><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/60px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png" decoding="async" width="45" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/120px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="354" /></span></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article may <b>lend <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view#Due_and_undue_weight" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">undue weight</a> to certain ideas, incidents, or controversies</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graffiti&amp;action=edit">help improve it</a> by rewriting it in a <b><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view#Balance" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">balanced fashion</a></b> that contextualizes different points of view.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2023</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbian</a> capital, <a href="/wiki/Belgrade" title="Belgrade">Belgrade</a>, the graffiti depicting a uniformed former <a href="/wiki/General_officer" title="General officer">general</a> of <a href="/wiki/Army_of_Republika_Srpska" title="Army of Republika Srpska">Serb army</a> and <a href="/wiki/War_Criminal" class="mw-redirect" title="War Criminal">war criminal</a>, convicted at <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Tribunal_for_the_former_Yugoslavia" title="International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia">ICTY</a> for war crimes and crimes against humanity, including <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_genocide" title="Bosnian genocide">genocide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing_in_the_Bosnian_War" title="Ethnic cleansing in the Bosnian War">ethnic cleansing</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_War" title="Bosnian War">Bosnian War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ratko_Mladi%C4%87" title="Ratko Mladić">Ratko Mladić</a>, appeared in a military salute alongside the words "General, thanks to your mother".<sup id="cite_ref-rferl.org_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rferl.org-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
Aleks Eror, Berlin-based journalist, explains how "veneration of historical and wartime figures" through street art is not a new phenomenon in the region of former Yugoslavia, and that "in most cases is firmly focused on the future, rather than retelling the past".<sup id="cite_ref-calvertjournal.com-Eror_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-calvertjournal.com-Eror-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Eror is not only analyst pointing to danger of such an expressions for the region's future. In a long expose on the subject of <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_genocide_denial" title="Bosnian genocide denial">Bosnian genocide denial</a>, at <a href="/w/index.php?title=Balkan_Diskurs&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Balkan Diskurs (page does not exist)">Balkan Diskurs</a> magazine and multimedia platform website, Kristina Gadže and Taylor Whitsell referred to these experiences as a young generations' "cultural heritage", in which youths are being exposed to the celebration and affirmation of war-criminals as part of their "formal education" and "inheritance".<sup id="cite_ref-balkandiskurs.com_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-balkandiskurs.com-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mural_u_Baru,_prikaz_ratnog_zlo%C4%8Dinca_Ratka_Mladi%C4%87a.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Mural_u_Baru%2C_prikaz_ratnog_zlo%C4%8Dinca_Ratka_Mladi%C4%87a.jpg/250px-Mural_u_Baru%2C_prikaz_ratnog_zlo%C4%8Dinca_Ratka_Mladi%C4%87a.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Mural_u_Baru%2C_prikaz_ratnog_zlo%C4%8Dinca_Ratka_Mladi%C4%87a.jpg/500px-Mural_u_Baru%2C_prikaz_ratnog_zlo%C4%8Dinca_Ratka_Mladi%C4%87a.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a><figcaption>Mural in <a href="/wiki/Bar,_Montenegro" title="Bar, Montenegro">Bar, Montenegro</a>, depicting the war criminal <a href="/wiki/Ratko_Mladi%C4%87" title="Ratko Mladić">Ratko Mladić</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>There are numerous examples of genocide denial through the celebration and affirmation of war criminals throughout the region of Western Balkans, inhabited by Serbs, using this form of artistic expression. Several more of these graffiti are found in the Serbian capital, and many more across Serbia and the Bosnian and Herzegovinian administrative entity, Republika Srpska, which is the ethnic Serbian majority enclave.<sup id="cite_ref-calvertjournal.com-Eror_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-calvertjournal.com-Eror-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ba.boell.org-Druško_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ba.boell.org-Druško-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Critics point that Serbia as a state, is willing to defend the mural of convicted war criminal, and have no intention to react on cases of genocide denial, noting that <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Internal_Affairs_(Serbia)" title="Ministry of Internal Affairs (Serbia)">Interior Minister of Serbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aleksandar_Vulin" title="Aleksandar Vulin">Aleksandar Vulin</a> decision to ban any gathering with an intent to remove the mural, with the deployment of riot police, sends the message of "tacit endorsement".<sup id="cite_ref-rferl.org_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rferl.org-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-europeanwesternbalkans.com-Popović_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-europeanwesternbalkans.com-Popović-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Consequently, on 9 November 2021, Serbian heavy police in riot gear, with the graffiti creators and their supporters,<sup id="cite_ref-balkandiskurs.com_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-balkandiskurs.com-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> blocked the access to the mural to prevent human rights groups and other activists to paint over it and mark the <a href="/wiki/International_Day_Against_Fascism_and_Antisemitism" class="mw-redirect" title="International Day Against Fascism and Antisemitism">International Day Against Fascism and Antisemitism</a> in that way,<sup id="cite_ref-rferl.org_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rferl.org-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and even arrested two civic activist for throwing eggs at the graffiti.<sup id="cite_ref-europeanwesternbalkans.com-Popović_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-europeanwesternbalkans.com-Popović-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Offensive_graffiti">Offensive graffiti</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Stop_graffitti.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Stop_graffitti.jpg/220px-Stop_graffitti.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Stop_graffitti.jpg/330px-Stop_graffitti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Stop_graffitti.jpg/440px-Stop_graffitti.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4272" data-file-height="2848" /></a><figcaption>Gang symbol markings on public property, <a href="/wiki/Millwood,_Washington" title="Millwood, Washington">Millwood, Washington</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Graffiti may also be used as an offensive expression. This form of graffiti may be difficult to identify, as it is mostly removed by the local authority (as councils which have adopted strategies of criminalization also strive to remove graffiti quickly).<sup id="cite_ref-halsey_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halsey-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Therefore, existing racist graffiti is mostly more subtle and at first sight, not easily recognized as "racist". It can then be understood only if one knows the relevant "local code" (social, historical, political, temporal, and spatial), which is seen as <a href="/wiki/Heteroglossia" title="Heteroglossia">heteroglot</a> and thus a 'unique set of conditions' in a cultural context.<sup id="cite_ref-holqu_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-holqu-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A spatial local code for example, could be that there is a certain youth group in an area that is engaging heavily in racist activities. So, for residents (knowing the local code), a graffiti containing only the name or abbreviation of this gang already is a racist expression, reminding the offended people of their gang activities. Also a graffiti is in most cases, the <a href="/wiki/Herald" title="Herald">herald</a> of more serious criminal activity to come.<sup id="cite_ref-kelling_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kelling-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A person who does not know these gang activities would not be able to recognize the meaning of this graffiti. Also if a tag of this youth group or gang is placed on a building occupied by asylum seekers, for example, its racist character is even stronger.
</p><p>By making the graffiti less explicit (as adapted to social and legal constraints),<sup id="cite_ref-barker_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-barker-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> these drawings are less likely to be removed, but do not lose their threatening and offensive character.<sup id="cite_ref-lynn_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lynn-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Elsewhere, activists in Russia have used painted <a href="/wiki/Caricature" title="Caricature">caricatures</a> of local officials with their mouths as <a href="/wiki/Pothole" title="Pothole">potholes</a>, to show their anger about the poor state of the roads.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a>, England, a graffitists painted obscene images around potholes, which often resulted in them being repaired within 48 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Decorative_and_high_art">Decorative and high art</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Street_art" title="Street art">Street art</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:JonesyLondon.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/JonesyLondon.jpg/250px-JonesyLondon.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="230" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/JonesyLondon.jpg/330px-JonesyLondon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/JonesyLondon.jpg/500px-JonesyLondon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2432" data-file-height="3286" /></a><figcaption>A bronze work by Jonesy on a wall in Brick Lane (<a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>). Diameter about 8&#160;cm.</figcaption></figure>
<p>In the early 1980s, the first art galleries to show graffitists to the public were <a href="/wiki/Fashion_Moda" title="Fashion Moda">Fashion Moda</a> in <a href="/wiki/The_Bronx" title="The Bronx">the Bronx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Now_Gallery" title="Now Gallery">Now Gallery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fun_Gallery" title="Fun Gallery">Fun Gallery</a>, both in the <a href="/wiki/East_Village,_Manhattan" title="East Village, Manhattan">East Village, Manhattan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-openedition-fashionmoda_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-openedition-fashionmoda-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hyperallergic-fashionmoda_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hyperallergic-fashionmoda-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nytimes-fashionmoda_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-fashionmoda-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nydaily-fashionmoda_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nydaily-fashionmoda-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A 2006 exhibition at the <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Museum" title="Brooklyn Museum">Brooklyn Museum</a> displayed graffiti as an art form that began in <a href="/wiki/Boroughs_of_New_York_City" title="Boroughs of New York City">New York's outer boroughs</a> and reached great heights in the early 1980s with the work of Crash, Lee, Daze, <a href="/wiki/Keith_Haring" title="Keith Haring">Keith Haring</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Michel_Basquiat" title="Jean-Michel Basquiat">Jean-Michel Basquiat</a>. It displayed 22 works by New York graffitists, including <a href="/wiki/Crash_(graffiti_artist)" class="mw-redirect" title="Crash (graffiti artist)">Crash</a>, Daze, and <a href="/wiki/Lady_Pink" title="Lady Pink">Lady Pink</a>. In an article about the exhibition in the magazine <i>Time Out</i>, curator Charlotta Kotik said that she hoped the exhibition would cause viewers to rethink their assumptions about graffiti.
</p><p>From the 1970s onwards, <a href="/wiki/Burhan_Do%C4%9Fan%C3%A7ay" title="Burhan Doğançay">Burhan Doğançay</a> photographed urban walls all over the world; these he then archived for use as sources of inspiration for his painterly works. The project today known as "Walls of the World" grew beyond even his own expectations and comprises about 30,000 individual images. It spans a period of 40 years across five continents and 114 countries. In 1982, photographs from this project comprised a one-man exhibition titled "Les murs murmurent, ils crient, ils chantent<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>..." (The walls whisper, shout and sing<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>...) at the <a href="/wiki/Centre_Pompidou" title="Centre Pompidou">Centre Georges Pompidou</a> in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>.
</p><p>In Australia, art historians have judged some local graffiti of sufficient creative merit to rank them firmly within the arts. <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Press" title="Oxford University Press">Oxford University Press</a>'s art history text <i>Australian Painting 1788–2000</i> concludes with a long discussion of graffiti's key place within contemporary <a href="/wiki/Visual_culture" title="Visual culture">visual culture</a>, including the work of several Australian practitioners.<sup id="cite_ref-smithea_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smithea-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Between March and April 2009, 150 artists exhibited 300 pieces of graffiti at the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Palais" title="Grand Palais">Grand Palais</a> in Paris.
<sup id="cite_ref-rfi_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfi-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rohter_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rohter-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
	<li class="gallerycaption">Street art graffiti</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 124px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 122px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Graffiti_(Budapest,_Pestszentl%C5%91rinc).jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Graffiti on a wall in Budapest, Hungary"><img alt="Graffiti on a wall in Budapest, Hungary" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Graffiti_%28Budapest%2C_Pestszentl%C5%91rinc%29.jpg/183px-Graffiti_%28Budapest%2C_Pestszentl%C5%91rinc%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="122" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Graffiti_%28Budapest%2C_Pestszentl%C5%91rinc%29.jpg/274px-Graffiti_%28Budapest%2C_Pestszentl%C5%91rinc%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Graffiti_%28Budapest%2C_Pestszentl%C5%91rinc%29.jpg/365px-Graffiti_%28Budapest%2C_Pestszentl%C5%91rinc%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2182" data-file-height="2151" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Graffiti on a wall in <a href="/wiki/Budapest" title="Budapest">Budapest</a>, Hungary</div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Graffiti_in_Tikkurila.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Graffiti on the wall of pedestrian tunnel in Tikkurila, Vantaa, Finland"><img alt="Graffiti on the wall of pedestrian tunnel in Tikkurila, Vantaa, Finland" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Graffiti_in_Tikkurila.jpg/330px-Graffiti_in_Tikkurila.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Graffiti_in_Tikkurila.jpg/500px-Graffiti_in_Tikkurila.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Graffiti_in_Tikkurila.jpg/960px-Graffiti_in_Tikkurila.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5597" data-file-height="3731" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Graffiti on the wall of pedestrian tunnel in <a href="/wiki/Tikkurila" title="Tikkurila">Tikkurila</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vantaa" title="Vantaa">Vantaa</a>, Finland</div>
		</li>
</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Environmental_effects">Environmental effects</h2></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Spray_paint" title="Spray paint">Spray paint</a> has many negative environmental effects. The paint contains toxic chemicals, and the can uses volatile hydrocarbon gases to spray the paint onto a surface.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compound" title="Volatile organic compound">Volatile organic compound</a> (VOC) leads to ground level ozone formation and most of graffiti related emissions are VOCs.<sup id="cite_ref-Leskys_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leskys-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A 2010 paper estimates 4,862 tons of VOCs were released in the United States in activities related to graffiti.<sup id="cite_ref-Leskys_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leskys-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EPA_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPA-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Government_responses">Government responses</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:US_military_anti-graffiti_poster_in_Kuwait.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/US_military_anti-graffiti_poster_in_Kuwait.png/170px-US_military_anti-graffiti_poster_in_Kuwait.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/US_military_anti-graffiti_poster_in_Kuwait.png/255px-US_military_anti-graffiti_poster_in_Kuwait.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/US_military_anti-graffiti_poster_in_Kuwait.png/340px-US_military_anti-graffiti_poster_in_Kuwait.png 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="630" /></a><figcaption>Poster at a US military base in <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a> decrying graffiti, itself having been graffitied</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Asia">Asia</h3></div>
<p>In China, <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a> in the 1920s used revolutionary slogans and paintings in public places to galvanize the country's communist movement.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Based on different national conditions, many people believe that China's attitude towards Graffiti is fierce, but in fact, according to Lance Crayon in his film <i>Spray Paint Beijing: Graffiti in the Capital of China</i>, Graffiti is generally accepted in <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>, with artists not seeing much police interference. Political and religiously sensitive graffiti, however, is not allowed.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In Hong Kong, <a href="/wiki/Tsang_Tsou_Choi" class="mw-redirect" title="Tsang Tsou Choi">Tsang Tsou Choi</a> was known as the <i>King of Kowloon</i> for his <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligraphy</a> graffiti over many years, in which he claimed ownership of the area. Now some of his work is preserved officially.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, the government has made some concessions to graffitists. Since 2005 they have been allowed to freely display their work along some sections of riverside retaining walls in designated "Graffiti Zones".<sup id="cite_ref-taitimes_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taitimes-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> From 2007, Taipei's department of cultural affairs also began permitting graffiti on fences around major public construction sites. Department head Yong-ping Lee (李永萍) stated, "We will promote graffiti starting with the public sector, and then later in the private sector too. It's our goal to beautify the city with graffiti". The government later helped organize a graffiti contest in <a href="/wiki/Ximending" title="Ximending">Ximending</a>, a popular shopping district. Graffitists caught working outside of these designated areas still face fines up to <a href="/wiki/NT$" class="mw-redirect" title="NT$">NT$</a>6,000 under a department of environmental protection regulation.<sup id="cite_ref-taitimes2_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taitimes2-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, Taiwanese authorities can be relatively lenient, one veteran police officer stating anonymously, "Unless someone complains about vandalism, we won't get involved. We don't go after it proactively."<sup id="cite_ref-reuters_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1993, after several expensive cars in <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> were spray-painted, the police arrested a student from the <a href="/wiki/Singapore_American_School" title="Singapore American School">Singapore American School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_P._Fay" class="mw-redirect" title="Michael P. Fay">Michael P. Fay</a>, questioned him, and subsequently charged him with vandalism. Fay pleaded guilty to vandalizing a car in addition to stealing road signs. Under the 1966 <a href="/wiki/Vandalism_Act_(Singapore)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vandalism Act (Singapore)">Vandalism Act of Singapore</a>, originally passed to curb the spread of communist graffiti in Singapore, the court sentenced him to four months in jail, a fine of S$3,500 (US$2,233), and a <a href="/wiki/Caning" title="Caning">caning</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> ran several editorials and op-eds that condemned the punishment and called on the American public to flood the Singaporean embassy with protests. Although the Singapore government received many calls for <a href="/wiki/Pardon" title="Pardon">clemency</a>, Fay's caning took place in Singapore on 5 May 1994. Fay had originally received a sentence of six strokes of the cane, but the presiding <a href="/wiki/President_of_Singapore" title="President of Singapore">president of Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ong_Teng_Cheong" title="Ong Teng Cheong">Ong Teng Cheong</a>, agreed to reduce his caning sentence to four lashes.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Korea" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of Korea">South Korea</a>, Park Jung-soo was fined two million <a href="/wiki/South_Korean_won" title="South Korean won">South Korean won</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a> Central District Court for spray-painting a rat on posters of the <a href="/wiki/G-20" class="mw-redirect" title="G-20">G-20</a> Summit a few days before the event in November 2011. Park alleged that the initial in "G-20" sounds like the Korean word for "rat", but Korean government prosecutors alleged that Park was making a derogatory statement about the president of South Korea, <a href="/wiki/Lee_Myung-bak" title="Lee Myung-bak">Lee Myung-bak</a>, the host of the summit. This case led to public outcry and debate on the lack of government tolerance and in support of freedom of expression. The court ruled that the painting, "an ominous creature like a rat" amounts to "an organized criminal activity" and upheld the fine while denying the prosecution's request for imprisonment for Park.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
	<li class="gallerycaption">Graffiti in Asia</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 92px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 90px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Tsang_graffiti.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Street graffiti in Hong Kong"><img alt="Street graffiti in Hong Kong" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Tsang_graffiti.jpg/135px-Tsang_graffiti.jpg" decoding="async" width="90" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Tsang_graffiti.jpg/202px-Tsang_graffiti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Tsang_graffiti.jpg/270px-Tsang_graffiti.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a></span></div>

			<div class="thumb" style="width: 180.66666666667px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:201712_Graffiti_on_a_building_of_Shentangqiao1.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="The Graffiti Piece &quot;Tante&quot; (by Chen Dongfan) on the surface wall of an old residential building in Hangzhou, Zhejiang, China"><img alt="The Graffiti Piece &quot;Tante&quot; (by Chen Dongfan) on the surface wall of an old residential building in Hangzhou, Zhejiang, China" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/201712_Graffiti_on_a_building_of_Shentangqiao1.jpg/330px-201712_Graffiti_on_a_building_of_Shentangqiao1.jpg" decoding="async" width="181" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/201712_Graffiti_on_a_building_of_Shentangqiao1.jpg/500px-201712_Graffiti_on_a_building_of_Shentangqiao1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/201712_Graffiti_on_a_building_of_Shentangqiao1.jpg/960px-201712_Graffiti_on_a_building_of_Shentangqiao1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4912" data-file-height="3264" /></a></span></div>

		</li>
</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Europe_2">Europe</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Graffiti_removal_berlin.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Graffiti_removal_berlin.jpg/170px-Graffiti_removal_berlin.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Graffiti_removal_berlin.jpg/255px-Graffiti_removal_berlin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Graffiti_removal_berlin.jpg/340px-Graffiti_removal_berlin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="3456" /></a><figcaption>Graffiti removal in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>In Europe, community cleaning squads have responded to graffiti, in some cases with reckless abandon, as when in 1992 in France a local Scout group, attempting to remove modern graffiti, damaged <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20171109194433/http://www.panoramio.com/photo/26895452">two prehistoric paintings</a><sup><a href="/wiki/Template:Usurped/doc" title="Template:Usurped/doc">[usurped]</a></sup> of <a href="/wiki/Bison" title="Bison">bison</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cave_of_Mayri%C3%A8re_sup%C3%A9rieure" class="mw-redirect" title="Cave of Mayrière supérieure">Cave of Mayrière supérieure</a> near the French village of <a href="/wiki/Bruniquel" title="Bruniquel">Bruniquel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tarn-et-Garonne" title="Tarn-et-Garonne">Tarn-et-Garonne</a>, earning them the 1992 <a href="/wiki/Ig_Nobel_Prize" title="Ig Nobel Prize">Ig Nobel Prize</a> in <a href="/wiki/Archeology" class="mw-redirect" title="Archeology">archeology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-improb_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-improb-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2006, the European Parliament directed the European Commission to create urban environment policies to prevent and eliminate dirt, litter, graffiti, animal excrement, and excessive noise from domestic and vehicular music systems in European cities, along with other concerns over urban life.<sup id="cite_ref-europa_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-europa-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Budapest" title="Budapest">Budapest</a>, Hungary, both a city-backed movement called <i>I Love Budapest</i> and a special police division tackle the problem, including the provision of approved areas.<sup id="cite_ref-index_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-index-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Graffiti_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Graffiti in the United Kingdom">Graffiti in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Leake_street_tunnel_2019-11-24.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Leake_street_tunnel_2019-11-24.jpg/250px-Leake_street_tunnel_2019-11-24.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Leake_street_tunnel_2019-11-24.jpg/330px-Leake_street_tunnel_2019-11-24.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Leake_street_tunnel_2019-11-24.jpg/500px-Leake_street_tunnel_2019-11-24.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456" /></a><figcaption>It is permitted to create graffiti in the <a href="/wiki/Leake_Street" title="Leake Street">Leake st</a> tunnel. The tunnel runs underneath Waterloo station in London. 2019</figcaption></figure>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Anti-social_Behaviour_Act_2003" title="Anti-social Behaviour Act 2003">Anti-social Behaviour Act 2003</a> became Britain's latest anti-graffiti legislation. In August 2004, the <a href="/wiki/Keep_Britain_Tidy" title="Keep Britain Tidy">Keep Britain Tidy</a> campaign issued a press release calling for <a href="/wiki/Zero_tolerance" title="Zero tolerance">zero tolerance</a> of graffiti and supporting proposals such as issuing "on the spot" <a href="/wiki/Fine_(penalty)" title="Fine (penalty)">fines</a> to graffiti offenders and banning the sale of aerosol paint to anyone under the age of 16.<sup id="cite_ref-encams_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-encams-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The press release also condemned the use of graffiti images in advertising and in <a href="/wiki/Music_video" title="Music video">music videos</a>, arguing that real-world experience of graffiti stood far removed from its often-portrayed "cool" or "edgy'" image.
</p><p>To back the campaign, 123 <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament_(United_Kingdom)" title="Member of Parliament (United Kingdom)">Members of Parliament</a> (MPs) (including then Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>), signed a charter which stated: "Graffiti is not art, it's crime. On behalf of my constituents, I will do all I can to rid our community of this problem."<sup id="cite_ref-wire_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wire-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the UK, city councils have the power to take action against the owner of any property that has been defaced under the <a href="/wiki/Anti-social_Behaviour_Act_2003" title="Anti-social Behaviour Act 2003">Anti-social Behaviour Act 2003</a> (as amended by the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.opsi.gov.uk/acts/acts2005/ukpga_20050016_en_1">Clean Neighbourhoods and Environment Act 2005</a>) or, in certain cases, the Highways Act. This is often used against owners of property that are complacent in allowing protective boards to be defaced so long as the property is not damaged.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In July 2008, a <a href="/wiki/Criminal_conspiracy" title="Criminal conspiracy">conspiracy</a> charge was used to convict graffitists for the first time. After a three-month police surveillance operation,<sup id="cite_ref-bbc2_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc2-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> nine members of the DPM crew were convicted of conspiracy to commit <a href="/wiki/Property_damage" title="Property damage">criminal damage</a> costing at least £1 million. Five of them received prison sentences, ranging from eighteen months to two years. The unprecedented scale of the investigation and the severity of the sentences rekindled public debate over whether graffiti should be considered art or crime.<sup id="cite_ref-indep_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-indep-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some councils, like those of Stroud and Loerrach, provide approved areas in the town where graffitists can showcase their talents, including underpasses, car parks, and walls that might otherwise prove a target for the "spray and run".<sup id="cite_ref-bbc3_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc3-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 87.333333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 85.333333333333px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Our_Lady_of_the_Hattifatteners_(cropped).jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Graffiti by Hazul in Porto, Portugal"><img alt="Graffiti by Hazul in Porto, Portugal" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Our_Lady_of_the_Hattifatteners_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-Our_Lady_of_the_Hattifatteners_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="86" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Our_Lady_of_the_Hattifatteners_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Our_Lady_of_the_Hattifatteners_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2542" data-file-height="3561" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Graffiti by <a href="/wiki/Hazul" title="Hazul">Hazul</a> in <a href="/wiki/Porto" title="Porto">Porto</a>, Portugal</div>
		</li>
</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Australia">Australia</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Graffiti_tunnel_2009,_University_of_Sydney.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Graffiti_tunnel_2009%2C_University_of_Sydney.jpg/220px-Graffiti_tunnel_2009%2C_University_of_Sydney.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Graffiti_tunnel_2009%2C_University_of_Sydney.jpg/330px-Graffiti_tunnel_2009%2C_University_of_Sydney.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Graffiti_tunnel_2009%2C_University_of_Sydney.jpg/440px-Graffiti_tunnel_2009%2C_University_of_Sydney.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a><figcaption>Graffiti Tunnel, University of Sydney at <a href="/wiki/Camperdown,_New_South_Wales" title="Camperdown, New South Wales">Camperdown</a> (2009)</figcaption></figure>
<p>Ancient rock art in Australia is seen as a sacred part of First Nations histories, and many of it is legally protected, and some are given National Heritage status.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Tangara_T63_with_graffiti.png" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="T set with graffiti" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Tangara_T63_with_graffiti.png/220px-Tangara_T63_with_graffiti.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="128" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Tangara_T63_with_graffiti.png/330px-Tangara_T63_with_graffiti.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Tangara_T63_with_graffiti.png/440px-Tangara_T63_with_graffiti.png 2x" data-file-width="1211" data-file-height="706" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Trains_T_set" title="Sydney Trains T set">Sydney Trains T set</a> at <a href="/wiki/Milsons_Point_railway_station" title="Milsons Point railway station">Milsons Point railway station</a> with graffiti</figcaption></figure>
<p>In an effort to reduce vandalism, many cities in Australia have designated walls or areas exclusively for use by writers. One early example is the "Graffiti Tunnel" located at the <a href="/wiki/Camperdown,_New_South_Wales" title="Camperdown, New South Wales">Camperdown</a> Campus of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Sydney" title="University of Sydney">University of Sydney</a>, which is available for use by any student at the university to tag, advertise, poster, and paint. Advocates of this idea suggest that this discourages petty vandalism yet encourages artists to take their time and produce great art, without worry of being caught or arrested for vandalism or <a href="/wiki/Trespassing" class="mw-redirect" title="Trespassing">trespassing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-warrin_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-warrin-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-abc_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abc-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Others disagree with this approach, arguing that the presence of legal graffiti walls does not demonstrably reduce illegal graffiti elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-towno_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-towno-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some local government areas throughout Australia have introduced "anti-graffiti squads", who clean graffiti in the area, and such crews as BCW (Buffers Can't Win) have taken steps to keep one step ahead of local graffiti cleaners.
</p><p>Many state governments have banned the sale or possession of spray paint to those under the age of 18 (<a href="/wiki/Age_of_majority" title="Age of majority">age of majority</a>). However, a number of local governments in Victoria have taken steps to recognize the cultural heritage value of some examples of graffiti, such as prominent political graffiti. Tough new graffiti laws have been introduced in Australia with fines of up to A$26,000 and two years in prison.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a> is a prominent graffiti city of Australia with many of its lanes being tourist attractions, such as <a href="/wiki/Hosier_Lane,_Melbourne" class="mw-redirect" title="Hosier Lane, Melbourne">Hosier Lane</a> in particular, a popular destination for photographers, wedding photography, and backdrops for corporate print advertising. The <a href="/wiki/Lonely_Planet" title="Lonely Planet">Lonely Planet</a> travel guide cites Melbourne's street as a major attraction. All forms of graffiti, including <a href="/wiki/Sticker" title="Sticker">sticker</a> art, <a href="/wiki/Poster" title="Poster">poster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stencil" title="Stencil">stencil</a> art, and <a href="/wiki/Wheatpasting" class="mw-redirect" title="Wheatpasting">wheatpasting</a>, can be found in many places throughout the city. Prominent street art precincts include; <a href="/wiki/Fitzroy,_Victoria" title="Fitzroy, Victoria">Fitzroy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Collingwood,_Victoria" title="Collingwood, Victoria">Collingwood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northcote,_Victoria" title="Northcote, Victoria">Northcote</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brunswick,_Victoria" title="Brunswick, Victoria">Brunswick</a>, <a href="/wiki/St_Kilda,_Victoria" title="St Kilda, Victoria">St. Kilda</a>, and the CBD, where stencil and sticker art is prominent. As one moves farther away from the city, mostly along suburban train lines, graffiti tags become more prominent. Many international artists such as Banksy have left their work in Melbourne and in early 2008 a <a href="/wiki/Perspex" class="mw-redirect" title="Perspex">perspex</a> screen was installed to prevent a Banksy stencil art piece from being destroyed, it has survived since 2003 through the respect of local street artists avoiding posting over it, although it has recently had paint tipped over it.<sup id="cite_ref-wato_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wato-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="New_Zealand">New Zealand</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Christchurch_Stock_Yards.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Christchurch_Stock_Yards.jpg/220px-Christchurch_Stock_Yards.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Christchurch_Stock_Yards.jpg/330px-Christchurch_Stock_Yards.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Christchurch_Stock_Yards.jpg/440px-Christchurch_Stock_Yards.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4752" data-file-height="3168" /></a><figcaption>Former Christchurch stock yards</figcaption></figure>
<p>In February 2008 <a href="/wiki/Helen_Clark" title="Helen Clark">Helen Clark</a>, the New Zealand prime minister at that time, announced a government crackdown on tagging and other forms of graffiti vandalism, describing it as a destructive crime representing an invasion of public and private property. New legislation subsequently adopted included a ban on the sale of paint spray cans to persons under 18 and increases in maximum fines for the offence from NZ$200 to NZ$2,000 or extended community service. The issue of tagging become a widely debated one following an incident in <a href="/wiki/Auckland" title="Auckland">Auckland</a> during January 2008 in which a middle-aged property owner stabbed one of two teenage taggers to death and was subsequently convicted of <a href="/wiki/Manslaughter" title="Manslaughter">manslaughter</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="United_States">United States</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Graffiti_in_the_United_States" title="Graffiti in the United States">Graffiti in the United States</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Elevator_graffiti.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Elevator_graffiti.jpg/250px-Elevator_graffiti.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Elevator_graffiti.jpg/330px-Elevator_graffiti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Elevator_graffiti.jpg/500px-Elevator_graffiti.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="3264" /></a><figcaption>An elevator position indicator with scratch graffiti</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Tracker_databases">Tracker databases</h4></div>
<p>Graffiti databases have increased in the past decade because they allow vandalism incidents to be fully documented against an offender and help the police and prosecution charge and prosecute offenders for multiple counts of vandalism. They also provide law enforcement the ability to rapidly search for an offender's moniker or tag in a simple, effective, and comprehensive way. These systems can also help track costs of damage to a city to help allocate an anti-graffiti budget. The theory is that when an offender is caught putting up graffiti, they are not just charged with one count of vandalism; they can be held accountable for all the other damage for which they are responsible. This has two main benefits for law enforcement. One, it sends a signal to the offenders that their vandalism is being tracked. Two, a city can seek restitution from offenders for all the damage that they have committed, not merely a single incident. These systems give law enforcement personnel real-time, street-level intelligence that allows them not only to focus on the worst graffiti offenders and their damage, but also to monitor potential gang violence that is associated with the graffiti.<sup id="cite_ref-crcp_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crcp-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Gang_injunctions">Gang injunctions</h4></div>
<p>Many restrictions of civil gang injunctions are designed to help address and protect the physical environment and limit graffiti. Provisions of gang injunctions include things such as restricting the possession of marker pens, spray paint cans, or other sharp objects capable of defacing private or public property; spray painting, or marking with marker pens, scratching, applying stickers, or otherwise applying graffiti on any public or private property, including, but not limited to the street, alley, residences, block walls, and fences, vehicles or any other real or personal property. Some injunctions contain wording that restricts damaging or vandalizing both public and private property, including but not limited to any vehicle, light fixture, door, fence, wall, gate, window, building, street sign, utility box, telephone box, tree, or power pole.<sup id="cite_ref-crcp2_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crcp2-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Hotlines_and_reward_programs">Hotlines and reward programs</h4></div>
<p>To help address many of these issues, many local jurisdictions have set up graffiti abatement hotlines, where citizens can call in and report vandalism and have it removed. San Diego's hotline receives more than 5,000 calls per year, in addition to reporting the graffiti, callers can learn more about prevention. One of the complaints about these hotlines is the response time; there is often a lag time between a property owner calling about the graffiti and its removal. The length of delay should be a consideration for any jurisdiction planning on operating a hotline. Local jurisdictions must convince the callers that their complaint of vandalism will be a priority and cleaned off right away. If the jurisdiction does not have the resources to respond to complaints in a timely manner, the value of the hotline diminishes. Crews must be able to respond to individual service calls made to the graffiti hotline as well as focus on cleanup near schools, parks, and major intersections and transit routes to have the biggest impact. Some cities offer a reward for information leading to the arrest and prosecution of suspects for tagging or graffiti related vandalism. The amount of the reward is based on the information provided, and the action taken.<sup id="cite_ref-lawtec_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lawtec-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Search_warrants">Search warrants</h4></div>
<p>When police obtain search warrants in connection with a vandalism investigation, they are often seeking judicial approval to look for items such as cans of spray paint and nozzles from other kinds of aerosol sprays; etching tools, or other sharp or pointed objects, which could be used to etch or scratch glass and other hard surfaces; permanent marking pens, markers, or paint sticks; evidence of membership or affiliation with any gang or tagging crew; paraphernalia including any reference to "(tagger's name)"; any drawings, writing, objects, or graffiti depicting taggers' names, initials, logos, monikers, slogans, or any mention of tagging crew membership; and any newspaper clippings relating to graffiti crime.<sup id="cite_ref-paladin_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paladin-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="In_media">In media</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Documentaries">Documentaries</h3></div>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/80_Blocks_from_Tiffany%27s" title="80 Blocks from Tiffany&#39;s">80 Blocks from Tiffany's</a></i> (1979), a rare glimpse of the late 1970s in New York City toward the end of the notorious South Bronx gangs, the documentary shows many aspects of the South Bronx's predominantly Puerto Rican community, including reformed gang members, current gang members, the police, and the community leaders who try to reach out to them.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Stations_of_the_Elevated" title="Stations of the Elevated">Stations of the Elevated</a></i> (1980), the earliest documentary about subway graffiti in New York City, with music by Charles Mingus</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Style_Wars" title="Style Wars">Style Wars</a></i> (1983), an early documentary on hip hop culture, made in New York City</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Piece_by_Piece_(2005_film)" title="Piece by Piece (2005 film)">Piece by Piece</a></i> (2005), a feature-length documentary on the history of San Francisco graffiti from the early 1980s</li>
<li><i>Infamy</i> (2005), a feature-length documentary about graffiti culture as told through the experiences of six well-known graffiti writers and a graffiti buffer</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/NEXT:_A_Primer_on_Urban_Painting" class="mw-redirect" title="NEXT: A Primer on Urban Painting">NEXT: A Primer on Urban Painting</a></i> (2005), a documentary about global graffiti culture</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/RASH_(film)" class="mw-redirect" title="RASH (film)">RASH</a></i> (2005), a feature documentary about Melbourne, Australia, and the artists who make it a living host for street art</li>
<li><i>Jisoe</i> (2007), a glimpse into the life of a <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>, Australia, graffiti writer shows the audience an example of graffiti in struggling Melbourne Areas.</li>
<li><i>Roadsworth: Crossing the Line</i> (2009), about Montréal artist Peter Gibson and his controversial stencil art on public roads</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Exit_Through_The_Gift_Shop" class="mw-redirect" title="Exit Through The Gift Shop">Exit Through The Gift Shop</a></i> (2010) was produced by the notorious artist <a href="/wiki/Banksy" title="Banksy">Banksy</a>. It tells the story of <a href="/wiki/Thierry_Guetta" class="mw-redirect" title="Thierry Guetta">Thierry Guetta</a>, a French immigrant in Los Angeles, and his obsession with street art; <a href="/wiki/Shepard_Fairey" title="Shepard Fairey">Shepard Fairey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Invader_(artist)" title="Invader (artist)">Invader</a>, whom Guetta discovers is his cousin, are also in the film.</li>
<li><i>Still on and non the wiser</i> (2011) is a ninety-minute-long documentation that accompanies the exhibition with the same name in the Kunsthalle Barmen of the <a href="/wiki/Von_der_Heydt-Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Von der Heydt-Museum">Von der Heydt-Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wuppertal" title="Wuppertal">Wuppertal</a> (Germany). It draws vivid portrayals of the artists by means of very personal interviews and also catches the creation process of the works before the exhibition was opened.<sup id="cite_ref-stillo_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stillo-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i>Graffiti Wars</i> (2011), a documentary detailing <a href="/wiki/King_Robbo" title="King Robbo">King Robbo</a>'s feud with Banksy as well as the authorities' differing attitude towards graffiti and <a href="/wiki/Street_art" title="Street art">street art</a><sup id="cite_ref-ch4_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ch4-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Dramas">Dramas</h3></div>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Wild_Style" title="Wild Style">Wild Style</a></i> (1983), about hip hop and graffiti culture in New York City</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Turk_182" title="Turk 182">Turk 182</a></i> (1985), about graffiti as political activism</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Bomb_the_System" title="Bomb the System">Bomb the System</a></i> (2002), about a crew of graffitists in modern-day New York City</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Quality_of_Life_(film)" title="Quality of Life (film)">Quality of Life</a></i> (2004) was shot in the Mission District of San Francisco, co-written by and starring a retired graffiti writer.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Wholetrain" title="Wholetrain">Wholetrain</a></i> (2006), a German film</li></ul>
